S, s/Es/ (plural forms Ss) (plural forms S's) (plural forms s's)

noun S or s is the nineteenth letter of the English alphabet.

S

abbreviation for South, Saturday and Sunday.

-s (plural forms ) (plural forms \optima) (plural forms 0\1\minion) (plural forms 0\) (plural forms \minion) (plural forms 0\noun) (plural forms \minion) (plural forms 0\An) (plural forms \optima) (plural forms 0\s\minion) (plural forms 0\) (plural forms (or) (plural forms \optima) (plural forms 0\es) is added to the third person singular of the present tense of regular verbs, for

2 is added to the end of a noun to form the plural, for example house becomes houses, and glass becomes glasses.

(plural forms \minion) (plural forms 0\noun) (plural forms \minion) (plural forms 0\An) (plural forms \optima) (plural forms 0\s\minion) (plural forms 0\) (plural forms (or) (plural forms \optima) (plural forms 0\es)

3 noun An s can be added to numbers, letters and some abbreviations to form plurals, for example I lived through the 1960s.

... NOTE that this is the standard usage, but 's is sometimes still used: the 1960's.

's

1 noun An 's is added to most singular nouns and some pronouns to show possession, for example the girl's hat and someone's house.

2 noun In informal language, 's stands for us when it appears attached to the verb let. Let's take the train.

3 noun In spoken language, 's is often used as the shortened form of is or has, for example he's at work, or he's gone home.

4 noun An 's is added to plural nouns that do not end in s to show possession, for example men's hats. Compare this with ' by itself, which is added to plural nouns that end in s, for example the cows' calves.

abbreviation for South Australia.

Sabbath/`sAb@T/

noun In some religions, the Sabbath is a day of the week that is set aside for rest and activities such as going to church. For most Christians, the Sabbath is Sunday and for Jews, it is Saturday.

Sabin vaccine/`sEIb@n vAksi:n/

noun Sabin vaccine is a medicine given to children to protect them against the disease polio.

sack/sAk/

1 noun A sack is a large bag made of thick, rough woven material and used for storing and transporting goods such as fruit and vegetables, rice, and fertiliser.

2 verb In informal language, if you say your employer sacks you, you mean that they tell you that you cannot work for them any longer, usually because your work is not good enough. The boss sacked Li Ling yesterdayshe used to spend the whole day chatting to friends on the phone.

3 get the sack In informal language, if you say that someone gets the sack or is given the sack, you mean that their employer tells them that they cannot work for them any longer. The employer dismisses them. Don't be late again for work or you might get the sack. My boss gave me the sack because he thought I was lazy, but I'm not.

4 hit the sack In informal language, if you say you hit the sack, you mean you go to bed. I'm tired. I think I'll hit the sack.

... WORD FAMILY noun sacking

sacrament/`sAkr@m@nt/

noun In the Christian religion, a sacrament is a religious ceremony such as communion or baptism.

sacred/`sEIkr@d/

1 adjective If something is sacred, it is holy or it has to do with God or gods. A sacred burial site was discovered when work on the new building began. We listened to some of Bach's sacred music.

2 adjective If something is sacred, it is very important to someone or greatly respected ~by them. Is nothing sacred in this house! How dare you go through my private files! He thinks it's his sacred duty to go drinking with the boys on Friday night.

sacrifice/`sAkr@fa:Is/

1 verb In some religions, if something such as a human being or animal is sacrificed, they are killed and offered to the gods in a religious ceremony.

2 verb If you sacrifice something, you give it up, usually in order to gain something else for yourself or for someone else. I would gladly sacrifice all my money for good health.

... WORD FAMILY noun sacrifice adjective sacrificial

sacrilege/`sAkr@lIdZ/

1 noun Sacrilege is showing disrespect for holy or sacred objects or places. Shouting inside a church is sacrilege.

2 noun In informal language, you can say it is sacrilege to speak disrespectfully about somebody or something important or well-known. Don't talk about our great leader like thatit's sacrilege!

... WORD FAMILY adjective sacrilegious

safe/sEIf/

1 adjective If something is safe, it is not dangerous. It will not cause injury, damage or loss. You can use the drill nowit's quite safe. These stairs are not safe for the elderly. Is it safe to leave my bag here? Joe's a safe driver. KEEP IN A ~SAFE PLACE

2 noun A safe is a strong metal box that you can keep important and valuable things in. I keep my will in the wall safe. My money and jewellery are in the safe.

3 in safe hands If someone or something is in safe hands, they are being looked after by someone who is reliable and will not be in any danger. It's a very good hospital so you'll be in safe hands.

4 on the safe side If you do something in order to be on the safe side, you do it so that you will avoid danger or an unpleasant experience if something unexpected happens. I don't think it will rain but I'll take an umbrella just to be on the safe sideI hate getting wet.

5 safe and sound If you say that someone or something is safe and sound, you mean that they have been in a situation that could have been dangerous and are now in a secure place where they cannot be harmed. The police rescue squad found the lost hikers safe and sound after their overnight ordeal.

2 verb If something sags, it becomes less in amount or strength. Their spirits sagged when they realised there was still another ten kilometres to walk.

3 salt away If someone salts away money, they don't spend it. They keep it or hoard it. Jo never spends any moneyshe must have salted away a fortune by now.

same/sEIm/

1 adjective If two things are the same, they are exactly alike. They are identical. I have the same jumper as you. Bill and Paul are the same age.

2 adjective Same is also used to show that you are referring to one thing, not to different ones. Her boyfriend this year is the same one that she had last year. We work in the same office.

3 adjective Same is also used to refer to something that has already been mentioned. Juan has an atrocious temper and his brother has exactly the same outbursts.

4 all the same to If you say that a situation is all the same to you, you mean that you do not mind particularly what its outcome will be. You will be content with whichever way the situation develops. Wingping said she didn't mind which project she started first. She said it was all the same to her.

5 same to you In informal language, if you say same to you to someone who has wished you well in something, you mean that you wish them the same as what they have wished you. 'Good luck in the exams, Lana.' 'Same to you, Peter.'

6 thanks all the same If you say thanks all the same to someone who has offered you something, you refuse their offer, but you thank them for making it. 'Do you want me to help you with this ironing?' 'I'll do it later, thanks all the same.'

7 the same If something is the same as it was a certain time ago, it hasn't changed at all. Mary looks exactly the same as she did twenty years ago.

... WORD FAMILY noun sameness

sample/`sAmp@l/

1 noun A sample of something is a small piece, section or quantity of it that shows you what it's like. I'd like a sample of this material to see if it matches my carpet.

2 noun A sample of something is a small amount of it that is tested or analysed to see what is in it. The doctor took a sample of my blood and also asked me for a urine sample.

save/sEIv/

1 verb If you save someone, you rescue them by helping them escape from a difficult or dangerous situation. Lifesavers save many lives each year on Sydney beaches.

2 verb If you save money, you spend less than you earn and you put the part you haven't spent in a safe place, such as a bank or credit union.

3 verb If you save something such as time, money or energy, you make sure that you are efficient in your actions so that you have time, money or energy to spare. If we both do the cleaning it will save time and we can leave a bit earlier. ...save petrol.

4 verb If you save something, you do not throw it out or destroy it. You keep it. I'd like to read that article latercould you save it for me, please?

5 savings Your savings consist of the money you have managed to save by not spending it. Usually you keep this money in a financial institution such as a bank or credit union. My savings amount to the great sum of $4.

6 savings account A savings account is an account held either at a bank or a credit union where interest is paid on the money saved and where money can be withdrawn at short notice.

say/sEI/

1 verb If you say something, you speak. I'm sorry, I didn't hear you. What did you say?

2a verb If you say something, you express yourself in words. Have you got anything to say about the accident?

b noun In informal language, your say is your opinion about something. Before we vote on the matter, has everybody had their say?

3 verb If you say something in a written form such as a letter, you express it in writing. He wrote to say that he would not be able to attend the wedding.

4 to say the least If you say something and then add to say the least, you mean that what you have said is an understatement. The situation is in fact much more extreme than you describe it to be. Taking a small boat out in that storm was foolish to say the least.

5 you can say that again In informal language, if you say you can say that again after someone has said something to you, you mean that you completely agree with them. 'Boy, it's hot today!' 'You can say that again.'

6 you don't say In informal language, if you reply You don't say to someone who has told you something, you mean that you know that fact already. 'David's been married three timeshe's a bit hard to get on with, you know!' 'You don't say!'

14 noun A set is a radio or television receiver. Put the keys on the TV set.

16 set in If something sets in, it looks as if it will continue for quite a while. We won't be able to leave for ages if the rain sets in.

17 set into If something is set into something else, it is fitted in such a way that it does not stick out. Ask the electrician to set the power box into the side wall.

18 set off If you set off something, you cause it to explode or go off. We hope they will not set off any more bombs. Be careful not to set off the burglar alarm when you go into the house.

If you set off on a journey, you begin it. We hope to set off bright and early tomorrow morning. They're setting off on a new life together.

19 set up If you set someone up with something, you provide them with it. His parents set him up with a car for his birthday.

(a) In informal language, if someone sets you up, they try to make you take the blame for something that you haven't done. Another informal way of saying this is that they frame you.

(b) If you set up something, you start ~it, build it or erect it. His grandfather set up the family business in 1908. ...set up a roadblock.

shall/SAl/

1 verb You use shall, usually in the first person, to emphasise that a particular thing will happen in the future. For example, I shall go with you if you like. or We shall all help you clean up.

2 verb You use shall when you are referring to something that you intend to do or that you are sure will happen in the future. For example, ~I'm not free now but I shall be free this afternoon.

3 verb You use shall in questions when you are asking someone's advice or opinion about something. For example, Shall I ring Kristin now? or Shall I pour you a drink?

show/S@U/ (<verb forms> shows, showed, shown or

1 verb If something shows that a particular thing is the case, it gives information that makes people understand or realise this. This picture of our house shows that it has a red roof. His poor exam result showed that he hadn't studied enough.

3 verb ~If you show someone how to do something, you demonstrate or explain it to them. I'll show you how to set the table. He'll show us the art of weaving.

4 verb If something shows, it is noticeable. It's a lovely colour but I think the dirt will show.

6 verb If you show a particular emotion or feeling, you behave in a way that allows others to see how you feel. He couldn't bear to show his soft heart. ...show affection.

8 noun A show is an exhibition or display of something. There's a spring flower show at the botanical gardens. He won't go to art shows.

9 noun If you make a show of something, you try to make others think it is true. The appearance of it matters more to you than the thing itself. He kept up a show of interest in us but was relieved when we left. She made a show of tidying her room.

10 show off If someone shows off, they behave in such a way as to make people notice them. At the restaurant John showed off his Italian.

11 show around If you show someone around, you take them with you to show them places of interest and how to find their own way. Joan will show you around the building. She showed us around the exhibition.

since/sIns/

In its most common meaning, since can be a <preposition>, a <conjunction> or an <adverb>, depending on the grammar of the sentence.

1 Since refers to the time between a particular time or event you are talking about and now. It means 'from that time to this'. I have been living in Sydney since 1970. It is a month since I last saw you. ~ We met at school and have been good friends ever since.

2 adverb Since can also mean at a particular point of time within the period of time you are talking about. He used to work in the bank but has since moved into real estate.

3 conjunction You can use since to mean 'because'. Since you can't drive to work, I'll pick you up and drive you there. We'll go to a fish restaurant since you don't like meat.

4 long since If something has long since happened, it happened a long time ago. The time for crying has long since passed.

so/s@U/

So is a word with many different meanings. The exact meaning in each case depends on the context.

1 adverb So is used to refer back to something that has already been mentioned. 'Are you going out tonight?' 'I think so.'

2 adverb So is used to agree with a statement that has just been made. 'I thought he was overseas.' 'So did I.'

3 adverb So means 'in the way described'. 'It never stops raining in England.' 'Is that so?'

4 adverb So also means 'therefore'. It was late, and so we went to bed. He was hungry so I gave him a sandwich.

5 adverb So is also used to emphasise the amount or extent of something. It is so cold. Don't walk so fast. He seems so very happy.

6 and so on or and so forth You use and so on or and so forth at the end of a list to indicate that there are other items that you could have mentioned. At the barbecue we will need meat, plates, knives and forks, glasses, and so on. ...teachers, students, old students, families, and so forth.

7 or so You use or so to indicate that something such as an amount, a date or an estimate is only approximate. The amount is not precise. His exams are in a week or so. There were a hundred or so people at the conference.

8 so that or so as (a) So that and so as are used to explain the reason for something. Take your raincoat so that you don't get wet. Shouldn't you lock the house so as to avoid being burgled?

(b) You can use so...that or so...as to emphasise the degree of something and the result or consequence ~of it. She looked so pretty that he fell in love with her at first sight. You have been so kind as to make us feel a debt to you.

9 so what? In informal language, if you reply so what? to someone, you mean that what they have just said is not important. 'You came home very late last night.' 'So what?'

soak/s@Uk/

1 verb If you soak something, you put it in water or a liquid and leave it there for a period of time, usually because it is stained or very dirty. He soaked his muddy socks overnight.

2 verb If a liquid soaks something, it makes it very wet. We were totally soaked by the sudden rain.

3 noun In informal language, a soak is a person who drinks far too much alcohol. Harry's an old soak.

4 soak up If a dry substance soaks up a liquid, it draws the liquid into itself. You can also say that something is soaked up if it is used up. The sponge soaked up the spilled milk. The building project is soaking up all their money.

soon/su:n/

1 adverb You use soon to mean 'within a short time'. If something happens soon after a particular time, it happens a short time after it. Pick up your toyswe'll be leaving soon. Soon after we bought our house we had to repair the roof.

2 as soon as If you say that something will happen as soon as something else happens, you mean that the second thing will happen very quickly or immediately after the first. I'll pay the bill just as soon as I get your cheque.

3 as soon as possible If you say you'll do something as soon as possible, you mean that you will do it when you are able to do it. You may need to first finish doing something else that is more important. Mr Davies is with a client right now, but he'll phone you as soon as possible.

4 no sooner...than If you say that no sooner has one thing happened than another thing happens, you mean that the second thing happens straight after the first one. No sooner had I hung up than the phone rang again. No sooner had we gone outside than it started to rain.

5 sooner or later If you say that something will happen sooner or later, you mean that it might be a long time before it happens, but it will happen eventually. The truth about his financial affairs will emerge sooner or later. Sooner or later we'll have to buy a new carthis one's too unreliable.

6 the sooner the better If you say the sooner something happens the better, you mean that you think it would be good if it happened as soon as ~possible. The sooner he gives up smoking the better. 'When do you want me to start.' 'The sooner the better.'

b verb If you sponge something, you wipe it with a wet sponge to clean it. James sponges his car once a week.

2 noun A sponge is also a very light cake made from butter, flour, sugar and eggs.

3 verb In informal language, if you say that someone sponges off someone, you mean that they take money or the things they need to live from them without paying or doing anything in return. Larry's spongeing off his sister now that he's out of work, and has no intention of ever paying her back.

4 throw in the sponge In informal language, if you say that someone has thrown in the sponge, you mean that they have stopped trying. They have given up.

spontaneous/spOn`tEIni:@s/

1 adjective If something that a person does is spontaneous, it is done by them because they suddenly want to do it. They do not plan or arrange to do it. Spontaneous applause greeted the conductor as he walked out onto the stage.

2 adjective An event that is spontaneous occurs because of changes within some material or substance rather than being caused by something outside it. Spontaneous bushfires are very common, but this one was deliberately lit. ...a spontaneous chemical reaction.

3 adjective If someone is spontaneous, they are lively and natural. They do and say things as they occur to ~them. Everyone likes Lisa because of her friendly and spontaneous personality.

... WORD FAMILY noun spontaneity

1 noun A sport is a physical game such as tennis, football or cricket. People play it themselves or pay to watch others play.

2 noun If you say that someone is a sport or a good sport, you mean that they are fair and good-natured or that they accept defeat in a good-natured way. Jan's a good sportshe'll let you borrow her car if she doesn't need it herself. Wally was such a sport about losing the match.

spot/spOt/ (verb forms spots) (verb forms spotted) (verb forms spotting)

1 noun A spot is a small, round mark on something. Her dress was blue with white spots. There's a spot ~of sauce on your chin.

2 noun A spot can also be a red mark on the body, such as a pimple or part of a rash. He's covered in spots from his allergy.

3 noun A spot is a particular place. This is the spot where the accident happened. I know a nice spot for a picnic.

4 verb If you spot something, you notice it. She thought she spotted his face in the crowd.

5 in a spot or in a tight spot If you are in a spot or in a tight spot, you are in a difficult situation. Sandy got in a spot when she locked herself out of the house. I'm in a tight spot at the momentcan you lend me some money?

6 on the spot (a) If you are on the spot, you are there when something is happening. I was on the spot when the fire started.

(b) If you do something on the spot, you do it right away. They saw a house they liked and made up their minds on the spot. (c) If someone puts you on the spot, they put you in a difficult or embarrassing situation. She put me on the spot by asking me in front of everyone why I didn't like her.

7 spot check A spot check on something is a sudden, unplanned inspection of it or an inspection of a small number of items at random. When a health inspector arrived for a spot check of the premises, we were a bit worried. The manufacturer did a spot check and found that some of the brakes were faulty.

... WORD FAMILY adjective See spotted.adjective See spotty.

1 noun Spring is one of the four seasons of the year. It comes after winter and before summer.

2 noun A spring is a coil which bounces back into its original position after it has been stretched out.

3 verb If you spring, you jump up or forward quickly and suddenly. She sprang out of bed to answer the phone. We sprang to our feet when it started to rain.

4 noun A spring is a place where there is water coming up through the ground. They followed the creek bed trying to find the spring. Bathing in hot springs is sheer bliss.

5 verb If something springs somewhere, it moves suddenly and quickly. The glovebox keeps springing openwe need to get it fixed.

6 If one thing springs from or out of another, it starts from it or is the result of it. Her weight problem springs from an unhappy childhood. Their argument sprang out of a misunderstanding.

7 verb If you spring something on someone, you surprise them by telling them something they do not expect to ~hear. At dinner they sprang the news of their separation on us. The media sprang the story just before an election.

8 spring up If something springs up, it suddenly or quickly appears or develops. Flowers were springing up everywhere. The store continues to expandnew branches have sprung up in most country centres.

square/skwE@/

1a noun A square is a shape with four equal sides which are all at right angles to each other.

... NOTE that a right angle is 90 degrees.b noun If something is square, it has the shape of a square. The papers were stacked in square boxes. 2 noun In a town or city a square is an open area, often in the shape of a square, where people can sit or stand. Let's meet at the cafe in the town square.

3 adjective You use square before units of length when you state the area of something. For example, if a room is four metres wide and five metres long, its area is twenty square metres. Our block of land is seventy square metres.

4 noun In mathematics, the square of a number is that number multiplied by itself. The square of five is twenty-five.

5 adjective If you are square with someone, things are fair and equal between you. Here's $5now we're square. I have to beat him in chess one more time before we're square.

6 adjective A square meal is large and well-balanced. The children haven't had a square meal all daythey've only been eating snacks.

7 back to square one If you are back to square one, you have to start some process all over again because you were not successful with it the first time. We're back to square onewe'll have to advertise the job again because the new chap has just resigned.

8 square peg in a round hole If you say that someone is a square peg in a round hole, you mean that they are not well suited to a particular social scene or role. Jim feels like a square peg in a round hole with his work mateshe doesn't share their interest in sport.

9 square root The square root of a number is the number which, when multiplied by itself, equals the given number. The square root of four is two.

stand/stAnd/ (verb forms stands) (verb forms stood) (verb forms standing)

1 verb When you are standing, your body is in an upright position, your legs are straight, and your feet support the weight of your body. They had to stand for a while until seats became vacant.

2 verb When you stand or stand up, you move your body so that you are standing rather than sitting. You get up. Garth didn't bother to stand when I introduced my friend to him. The class stood up when the teacher came into the room.

3 verb If you stand back or stand aside, you move back or to the side a little. He stood aside to allow people to get past him. We lit the fireworks and stood back to watch them go off.

4 verb If something stands somewhere, it is situated there. It is in an upright position in that place. The church stands on the corner of Jones and Kippax Streets. A tall vase stood on the table.

5 verb If you stand an object somewhere, you place it there in an upright position. He stood the picture against the wall.

6 noun A stand is a small shop or stall, usually outdoors, where you can buy things such as drinks, ice-creams or newspapers. I bought an etching from a stand at an antique fair.

7 noun A stand is an object that is made to hold or support a particular thing. ...an umbrella stand. ...a cake stand.

8 noun A stand is a structure with seats or benches where spectators can sit to watch sporting events. The stands were filled to capacity throughout the cricket season.

9 verb If an offer or a decision stands, it continues to be valid. It does not change. Does the discount you were offering last week still stand?

10 verb If you cannot stand something, you cannot tolerate it or you dislike it very much. He couldn't stand the pain any longer. I can't stand rock music.

11 verb You can use stand instead of is when you are describing a particular condition or situation. I stand convinced he's not telling the truth. We'll have to retrench some staff if things continue as they stand. His office has stood empty since he quit.

12 verb If you ask someone how or where they stand on a particular issue, you are asking them for their opinion or attitude about it. Can you tell us where you stand on the issue of youth unemployment?

13 verb If you stand to gain something, you are likely to gain it. If you stand to lose something, you are likely to lose it. Robyn stands to make a huge profit ~out of the sale. Larry stands to lose friends if he keeps offending people.

14 verb If you stand in an election, you show that you wish to be voted into a particular position. You are a candidate for the position. Why don't you stand as our local representative? We need someone like you. Who else is standing for mayor?

15 stand by (a) If you stand by, you're ready to do something such as provide help if it becomes necessary. The instructor stood by in case we needed help with our assignment.

(b) If you stand by someone, you help and support them in difficult times. His friends stood by him when he lost his job.

(c) If you stand by something you have decided or promised to do, you continue with your intention to do it. You do not change your mind.

16 stand down If you stand down from a position or appointment, you resign from it. The president was asked to stand down.

17 stand for If you say that a letter stands for a particular word, you mean that it is an abbreviation for that word. M.U. stands for Macquarie University.

18 stand in If you stand in for someone, you do their job for them for a short time. Jane stood in for me as treasurer while I was away.

19 stand out If something stands out, it is very noticeable or remarkable in some way. Suzy's red hair makes her stand out.

20 stand up (a) If you stand up to someone, you defend yourself against their unfair or unreasonable treatment of you. He's not strong enough to stand up to the boss.

(b) If you stand up for someone or something, you defend them against someone who criticises or attacks them. You need to learn to stand up for yourself. Who will stand up for the rights of the poor?

21 take a stand If you take a stand or make a stand, you are ready to fight or argue against anyone who attacks you or tries to make you change your mind. He's really taking a stand on the issue of racism.

standard/`stAnd@d/

1 noun A standard is a level of quality that you can compare things with, especially a level that is generally considered to be acceptable. Helen's work is always of a high standard.

2 noun A standard is also something you use to test or measure the quality, size, weight, and so on, of something else. Fran's standard of fitness is too low for skiing. By world standards, Australians ~eat well. We'll have to raise the standard of our products if we're to stay in business.

3 adjective You use standard to describe something that is normal or generally accepted, rather than unusual or special. It's our standard procedure to get candidates to fill in this form before an interview. Do you speak standard Italian or a dialect? What's the standard text for first year psychology?

4 standards Standards are moral principles that are generally accepted as a guide for people's behaviour. His ruthless actions ignore all moral standards.

2 verb If someone stirs, they move slightly. The baby stirred briefly and then went back to sleep.

3 verb If the wind stirs an object, it causes it to move a little. The leaves were stirring in the breeze.

4 verb If something stirs you, it makes you feel excitement or some other strong emotion. The audience was stirred by the brilliance of the performance. The appalling conditions in the hospital stirred him deeply and he decided to do something about it.

5 verb In informal language, if you say that someone is stirring or stirring someone, you mean that they are teasing or deliberately trying to cause an argument. Don't listen to Tom, he's just stirring.

6 noun If something causes a stir, it excites, angers or shocks people. His purple suit caused quite a stir. Julie created a stir with the announcement of her resignation.

stop/stOp/ (verb forms stops) (verb forms stopped) (verb forms stopping)

1 verb If you stop doing something that you have been doing, you no longer do it. You cease doing it. They stopped work for morning tea. Stop making that noiseit's giving me a headache.

b noun If you put a stop to something, you prevent it from happening or continuing to happen. I want you to put a stop to ~all this complaining and get on with the job.

3 verb If a process or an activity stops, it finishes. It comes to an end. As soon as the rain stops, we'll go shopping. The man's breathing had stopped and they tried in vain to revive him. Suddenly the music stopped and the lights went out.

4a verb If someone or something that is moving stops, or if something stops them, they no longer move. She stopped briefly to look at the display in the shop window. The police were stopping cars to do breath tests on the drivers. Does this bus stop at Raglan Street?

b noun If something that is moving comes to a stop, it slows down and no longer moves. A big van pulled up and came to a stop in the driveway. Bill brought the horse to a stop after a short struggle.

6 verb If a device such as a clock stops or if you stop it, it no longer works or is switched off. My watch has stoppedthe battery must be dead. Kim stopped the engine to let it cool down.

7 verb If you stop somewhere, you stay there for a while before you continue to travel or move somewhere else. We have to stop at the bottle shop to get wine for the party.

8 verb If you stop a hole or something that is leaking, you close it off or seal it. He stopped the hole in the radiator with gum.

9 full stop A full stop is the punctuation mark that you use in written English at the end of a sentence.

strike/stra:Ik/ (verb forms strikes) (verb forms struck) (verb forms striking)

1 noun A strike is a period of time when workers stop working, usually because they want to get better pay or conditions. There was chaos in the city during the transport workers' strike. When the teachers went on strike, I had to stay at home to mind the children.

2 verb If you strike someone or something, you hit them deliberately. He struck the intruder with a wooden stick. A beginner at golf, he found it hard to strike the ballhe kept missing it. The pianist was striking the keys with energy and zeal.

3 verb If something strikes something else or strikes against it, it hits it. It collides with it. His head struck the windscreen and he was knocked unconscious. In the accident he struck his head against the windscreen. The tree was struck by lightning.

4 verb If something bad such as illness or trouble strikes, it happens ~suddenly. He was struck by cancer at the peak of his career. The cyclone struck the islands at midnight.

5 verb If a thought strikes you, it comes into your mind suddenly. It struck him that she wasn't telling the truth. It finally struck me why he had been so quiet all evening.

6 verb If something strikes you, it catches your attention or impresses you a lot. What struck him most about the dog was its long ears. Stef was quite struck by his charm and intelligence.

7 verb When a clock strikes, it makes a sound to indicate what time it is. When the clock strikes five everyone rushes home.

8 verb If you strike a bargain or a deal with someone, you come to an agreement with them. They've just struck a million-dollar deal with a major exporter.

9 verb If you strike a match, you make it produce a flame.

10 verb If someone strikes gold or oil, they discover it in the ground. The old prospector was still hoping to strike gold.

11 strike back If you strike back, you try to hurt someone because they have hurt you. When the boy punched him, little Matthew struck back with equal force.

12 strike down If something strikes a person down, it kills them or injures them seriously. He was struck down with cancer. Many of our soldiers have been struck down by the enemy.

13 strike off If a doctor or lawyer is struck off, their name is taken off an official list of doctors or lawyers and they are not allowed to continue their medical or legal work.

14 strike up If you strike up a conversation or a friendship with someone, you start one. At the party Jane struck up a conversation with a couple from Fiji.

4 verb If you suffer an experience that is painful or unpleasant, you are in a situation in which it happens to you. You ~go through it. You put up with it. The army suffered heavy losses in the battle. If you don't take my advice you'll have to suffer the consequences. He suffered the rough ride without complaining.

5 verb If something suffers, for example because it has been neglected, its quality or condition becomes worse. It deteriorates. His relationship with his family has suffered because he has been away a lot. While she was in prison her health suffered. Sales have been suffering since they put up their prices.

... WORD FAMILY noun See suffering.noun sufferer The new drug offers relief to headache sufferers.

supposed/s@`p@Uzd/

1 adjective You can describe something as supposed if you doubt that it is true or that it exists, even though people generally believe that it is true or that it exists. He's back at work after his supposed illness. The supposed building inspector was really a burglar.

2 be supposed (a) If you say that something is supposed to be the case, you mean that people believe it is true, but you do not know if it is definitely true. Children are supposed to need more sleep than adults. It's supposed to be the best restaurant in the arealet's try it.

(b) If you say that something is supposed to be done, you mean that it ought to be done, because of some rule, requirement, or custom. According to the building contract they're supposed to finish the house by June. How many of these pills am I supposed to take? Excuse me, you're not supposed to eat in the library.

(c) If you say that something is supposed to happen, you mean that someone intended it to happen, but it may not happen. It's supposed to be easy to assemble, but I've been struggling with it for an hour. I wonder where he ishe's supposed to have arrived by now. How is this gadget supposed to work?

sure/S@:/

1 adjective If you are sure that something is the case, you are certain or convinced that it is true. I'm sure it's going to rain. Are you sure you can do it? We're not sure of the reliability of this data. I'm not sure I like that idea. Jane's not sure she likes her job any more.

2 adjective If you say that someone is sure of achieving something or getting something, you mean that they will certainly achieve or get it. If you study a little every day you can be sure of passing the test. Thanks to the rain we can be sure of a good crop this year.

5 interjection In informal language, you can use sure instead of yes when you are replying to someone's request. 'Can I please borrow your car?' 'Sure.'

6 make sure (a) If you make sure that something is as you want it to be, you check or find out if it is definitely ~that way. Can you make sure I've turned off the iron, please. He made sure there were no cars in sight before he did the U-turn.

(b) If you make sure that something happens, you do something so that it will definitely happen. You ensure that it happens. Jim made sure the staff had plenty to do while he was awayhe gave them a new project to work on. Make sure the dough rises before you bake it.

3 verb If you survive someone, you continue to live after they have died. She survived her elder sister by four years.

4 verb If you survive an unpleasant experience, you manage to get through it without it affecting you too badly. Can you survive another evening in his company? 'I hate injections.' 'You'll survive!'
